             <div class="inset-container Mx-a">
              <div class="GridTable">
               <div class="D-tbr row-template">
                <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t col-left">

                   <div class="D-tbr row-content">
                    <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t col-left Pend-20" style="width:305px;max-width:305px;">
                     <h1 class="Font-A-6-110 title W-100" id="article-aft-title-114420372381">
                      Robert Plant and Jack White Make Lemonade Out of Led Zep's 'The Lemon Song'
                     </h1>
                     <div class="attribution-component D-b W-100" id="article-aft-attribution-114420372381" style="max-width:305px;">
                      <div class="author-info author-info-text-first Pt-10 W-100 Bd-t Bd-b Bdstart-0 Bdend-0">
                       <div class="GridTable W-100">
                        <div class="D-tbr">
                         <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t">
                          <cite class="D-b Fs-n Pb-14 byline col">
                           <span class="D-b author-name col-left">
                            <a class="Font-C-2-140 Fw-b" data-action-outcome="navigate" data-routable-url="/music/author/craig-rosen" data-ylk="t1:a2;t2:article;t3:cat;t8:647f96ca-f032-3922-8727-b34dab24e164;t9:16;elm:itm;elmt:cat;itc:0" href="/music/author/craig-rosen" rel="author" style="color:#000;">
                             Craig Rosen
                            </a>
                           </span>
                           <span class="D-b Font-C-2-140 Fw-n author-title">
                            Writer
                           </span>
                           <time class="D-b Font-C-2-140 Fw-n date">
                            <span>
                             March 23, 2015
                            </span>
                           </time>
                           <div class="follow Mt-4">
                            <iframe allowtransparency="true" frameborder="0" scrolling="no" src="//platform.twitter.com/widgets/follow_button.html?screen_name=CraigRosen&amp;show_screen_name=false&amp;show_count=false" style="border:none;overflow:hidden;width:60px;height:20px;">
                            </iframe>
                           </div>
                          </cite>
                         </div>
                        </div>
                       </div>
                      </div>
                     </div>
                    </div>
                    <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t col-right" style="max-width:500px;">
                     <div class="text-body-wrapper W-100 Font-B-1-160">
                      <noscript>
                      </noscript>
                      <div class="text-body W-100 Font-B-1-160" id="article-aft-text-body-114420372381" style="color:#222222;max-width:500px;">
                       <div class="body read-more-cut">
                        <div class="pre-cut" style="height:auto;margin-bottom:1.5em;">
                         <p>
                          Robert Plant may have balked at a full-scale Led Zeppelin tour after the bands historic 2007 one-off reunion at the O2 Arena, but he has no problem rocking the occasional Zep tune live.
                         </p>
                         <figure>
                          <iframe data-src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/UeItSpR2134?feature=oembed&amp;enablejsapi=1&amp;origin=https://safe.txmblr.com&amp;wmode=opaque" height="281" width="500">
                          </iframe>
                         </figure>
                         <p>
                          On Saturday night, Plant joined Jack White on stage for a blistering take of Led Zeppelins The Lemon Song during the former White Stripes singers headlining set at Lollapalooza Argentina. White has made a career of delivering his hard-rockin take on the blues, first in the White Stripes and now as a solo artist, and has covered the
                          <i>
                           Led Zeppelin II
                          </i>
                          album track in the past  last year when he topped the bill at Bonnaroo and also on his tour backing his second solo set,
                          <i>
                           Lazaretto
                          </i>
                          .
                         </p>
                         <figure>
                          <img alt="Robert Plant and Jack White Make Lemonade Out of Led Zep's 'The Lemon Song'" height="750" src="https://s.yimg.com/cd/resizer/2.0/FIT_TO_WIDTH-w500/fcf28517b1941c20e5adbae66fc6ff066cc3daee.jpg" width="500"/>
                         </figure>
                         <p>
                          For Plant, it was more of a rarity. According to Setlist.fm, its the first time hes played the track since he and Zep guitaristJimmy Page did it in 1995 in Norway on their tour to support
                          <i>
                           No Quarter: Jimmy Page and Robert Plant Unledded
                          </i>
                          , although that track was not included on that album.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          The fact that Plant dug into the Zep catalog with White  and still sounds good belting out the blues  was probably a minor annoyance for Page, Zep bassist/keyboardist John Paul Jones, and drummer Jason Bonham, the son of the bands founding drummer, John Bonham, all of whom were pushing hard for a full-scale Led Zeppelin tour following the 2007 reunion. Plant has reportedly balked at huge sums of money thrown at the band.
                          <i>
                           The Mirror
                          </i>
                          once reported that Richard Branson had offered the band $800 million to tour, although Plant called the report rubbish and Branson said there was no truth to the story.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          What is true is that Plant would rather follow his own muse than revive Led Zeppelin. He released his 10th solo album, the rootsy
                          <i>
                           Lullaby and The Ceaseless Roar
                          </i>
                          , back in November and is now touring with his current band, the Sensational Space Shifters, while Page continues to remaster and promote reissues of the Led Zeppelin catalog.
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          Its not the first time that White has gotten his jam on with a member of Led Zeppelin. In the 2008 rockumentary
                          <i>
                           It Might Get Loud
                          </i>
                          , White, Page, and U2s The Edge convened to share stories, inspiration and guitar tips and rock out on some tunes, including Led Zeppelins Kashmir and In My Time of Dying.
                         </p>
                         <figure>
                          <iframe data-src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/ODidAgdL40Y?feature=oembed&amp;enablejsapi=1&amp;origin=https://safe.txmblr.com&amp;wmode=opaque" height="281" width="500">
                          </iframe>
                         </figure>
                         <figure>
                          <iframe data-src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/hZlCz1hH-io?feature=oembed&amp;enablejsapi=1&amp;origin=https://safe.txmblr.com&amp;wmode=opaque" height="374" width="500">
                          </iframe>
                         </figure>
                         <p>
                          Since White has played with Page and now Plant, perhaps John Paul Jones should be expecting his call soon.
                         </p>
                        </div>
                        <div class="post-cut W-100" id="post-cut" style="transition-property:none;-webkit-transition-property:none;transition-duration:10s;-webkit-transition-duration:10s;transition-timing-function:ease-in;-webkit-transition-timing-function:ease-in;overflow:hidden;max-height:999999px;max-width:500px;">
                        </div>
                       </div>
                      </div>
                     </div>

the backstage shenanigans, the onstage mishaps, the misplaced money, and more.","body":"\u003Ci\u003Ephoto: Getty Images\u002FHulton Archive\u003C\u002Fi\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThey were the world. They were the rock &#x2018;n&#x2019; children. Hard as it may be to believe, it was 30 long years ago, on July 13, 1985, that more than 60 artists, 170,000 concertgoers, and a whopping 1.9 billion television viewers across 150 nations gathered for Sir Bob Geldof&#x2019;s massive Live Aid &#x201C;superconcerts&#x201D; &#x2014; held jointly at London&#x2019;s Wembley Stadium and Philadelphia&#x2019;s John F. Kennedy Stadium, and inspired by the all-star charity singles &#x201C;Do They Know It&#x2019;s Christmas?&#x201D; and &#x201C;We Are the World,&#x201D; to raise funds for Ethiopian famine relief. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cb\u003E\u003Cp\u003E[Related: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Fmusic\u002Fmidge-ure-the-man-behind-the-band-aid-mixing-103594511481.html\"\u003EMidge Ure: The Man Behind the Band Aid Mixing Board\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003C\u002Fb\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EThree decades later, where those funds went, exactly, is still a hot topic of debate. However, every &apos;80s child remembers tuning in to MTV, and every artist that graced those two revolving stages agrees that music history was made that day. Prince Charles and Princess Diana rubbed epauletted shoulders with David Bowie; Sean Penn (aka Mr. Madonna Ciccone at the time) hung out in a trailer with Simple Minds; Mick Jagger de-skirted Tina Turner on live television; the original &#x201C;Fab Five&#x201D; Duran Duran lineup played its last show for the next 18 years; Phil Collins hopped on the Concorde in order to appear at both concerts; Queen and U2 played gigs of a lifetime; and everyone from elder statesmen Page &amp; Plant, Bob Dylan, and Neil Young to new wave newbies Thomas Dolby and Howard Jones joined forces for a singalong heard &apos;round the world.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cb\u003E\u003Cp\u003E[Related: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Fmusic\u002Fphoto-ap-photo-we-are-the-world-associate-112944659161.html\"\u003E&apos;We Are the World&apos; Associate Producer Shares 11 Behind-the-Scenes Stories\u003C\u002Fa\u003E]\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \u003C\u002Fb\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBelow, a dozen or so Live Aid alumni from both sides of the pond reflect on that momentous day: the surreal star sightings, the nerves, the backstage shenanigans, the onstage mishaps, the misplaced money, and more.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMARTHA QUINN, MTV VJ (Philadelphia): \u003C\u002Fb\u003EMy contention is Live Aid 1985 was probably the pinnacle of rock &apos;n&#x2019; roll.&#xA0; \u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJOHN TAYLOR, DURAN DURAN\u002FPOWER STATION (Philadelphia):\u003C\u002Fb\u003E When I think back on Live Aid, I am almost overwhelmed by the glory of it all. Pop culture would never be the same again.\u003Cb\u003E \u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ESTEVE NORMAN, SPANDAU BALLET (London):\u003C\u002Fb\u003E It was an incredible atmosphere in Wembley Stadium. It was a very sunny day. Our singer had a full-length leather coat on! Even though it was a beautiful, sunny July day.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EADAM ANT (London):\u003C\u002Fb\u003E I did &#x201C;Viva La Rock,&#x201D; because I thought it was in the spirit of the day. But in retrospect, it wasn&#x2019;t a very pleasant experience for me &#x2014; because people would come up to me endlessly for 30 years saying, &#x201C;\u003Ci\u003EWhy\u003C\u002Fi\u003E didn&#x2019;t you do &apos;Stand and Deliver&#x2019;?&#x201D; Initially, I was asked to do four songs, and then I think what happened is big bands started to show interest &#x2014; Queen, Bowie &#x2014; and late in the day through Harvey Goldsmith, the promoter, I was told, &#x201C;You&#x2019;re off the show, you&#x2019;re not doing it.&#x201D; Fortunately, I was managed by Miles Copeland, who managed Sting, who said, &#x201C;If you don&#x2019;t put Adam on, you&#x2019;re not having Sting.&#x201D; So I got \u003Ci\u003Eone\u003C\u002Fi\u003E song.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FobXBhSraP3s\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ESTEVE NORMAN:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E There was a kind of tension that day, because we only had an allotted time, and if you went \u003Ci\u003Eone\u003C\u002Fi\u003E minute over, they pulled the plug on you. It was that simple. So I think everyone had their eye on the clock.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EADAM ANT:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E The main reason a lot of people got cut is when Bono jumped off the stage&#x2026; We were all told by Bob Geldof, &#x201C;No one goes over; if you don&#x2019;t stop, we&#x2019;re literally gonna press a button and the stage will spin around.&#x201D; And Bono jumped off the stage and played forever, so a lot of bands didn&#x2019;t get to play [their full sets]&#x2026;. Everybody stuck to their time except Bono! [\u003Ci\u003EEditor&apos;s note: Due to Bono&apos;s unplanned stage-jumping, U2 cut the final song from their set, &quot;Pride.&quot;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E]\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FGPeFMnu4WBA\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMICHAEL DES BARRES, THE POWER STATION (Philadelphia):\u003C\u002Fb\u003E Bill Graham was the master of the ceremonies, the P.T. Barnum of the situation. It was a circular stage, so you had a band or artist playing and when they finished their three obligatory songs, the thing would turn and then there would be another band set up, ready to go. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJIM KERR, SIMPLE MINDS (Philadelphia):\u003C\u002Fb\u003E I think we had 13 minutes or something. Thirteen unlucky minutes? But I remember this quite clearly: The legendary promoter Bill Graham came over and leaned in and made it \u003Ci\u003Eabundantly\u003C\u002Fi\u003E clear as to what would happen if we played just a minute over. I will just say that he was \u003Ci\u003Every\u003C\u002Fi\u003E frank. But we thought, &#x201C;This is cool! This is Bill Graham!&#x201D; Because he was such a legend on his own. You have to remember, we were just some guys from Glasgow.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMICHAEL DES BARRES\u003C\u002Fb\u003E: Andy [Taylor&#x2019;s] amp wasn&#x2019;t working and Bill said, &#x201C;You&#x2019;ll never eat lunch in this town in this town again,&#x201D; or whatever he said. And then the amp started to work and we finally went into the song; if you look at the footage, I&#x2019;m laughing my ass off at it.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FYfvTnQIL6F0\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJOHN OATES, HALL &amp; OATES (Philadelphia):\u003C\u002Fb\u003E I guess if you had to pinpoint a date that was the peak of the Hall &amp; Oates &apos;80s career, it would have been that day. Without a doubt. We actually headlined the show. When I say &#x201C;headline,&#x201D; there were a million superstars, but we were the last act to play [at Philly&#x2019;s JFK Stadium], so technically, I guess we headlined it. We did our own set first, then we brought out Eddie Kendricks and David Ruffin, who are two of the principal lead singers of the Temptations, who we had done &#x201C;Live at the Apollo&#x201D; with a few months before that. We did a Temptations medley and paid tribute to the Temptations through that. Mick Jagger was working on solo projects at the time outside of the Stones. He wanted to do something. He didn&#x2019;t have a band, so he reached out to us to be his backup band. He came out, then he brought Tina Turner out. So we were onstage for a long time, because we essentially did three sets. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FqK14lmxpZS0\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMARTHA QUINN: \u003C\u002Fb\u003ETina Turner canceled gigs and paid her own way to be there. Why did she do that? She said because Mick asked her to! Everybody was on hand to help everybody else. Hall &amp; Oates helping Mick Jagger. Madonna coming onstage with the Thompson Twins and Nile Rodgers. The Beach Boys backing up REO Speedwagon! It was just a kibbutz of talent.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002F-436Qxw6Nks\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FCTzA-Rydr0E\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJOHN OATES:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E One of the things I remember about it, prior to the actual show itself, was when we did rehearsal in New York at Studio Instrument Rentals on the soundstage. We rehearsed with Eddie and David, and then Mick came in to go over his stuff. He went into the entire Mick Jagger routine, but this was in a room with nobody except us. He started running around the stage, flapping his wings like a chicken and doing the Mick Jagger facial things, as if there were 100,000 people in the room. The room was empty. That was really amazing. He went into the full thing. He didn&#x2019;t just stand there and sing his songs like, &#x201C;Hey, it&#x2019;s a rehearsal. I&#x2019;m just going to kind of stroke my way through this.&#x201D; No, he did the whole thing. But of course, he didn&#x2019;t tell us he was going to rip Tina&#x2019;s skirt off later. That was a cool surprise.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMARTHA QUINN:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E That was the original wardrobe malfunction, when he tore off her skirt. But I have analyzed that footage, and I think it was very planned. Interestingly, it didn&#x2019;t get that much of a reaction, really. I think it was because it was within the world of rock &apos;n&#x2019; roll, so people were like, &#x201C;Oh, whatever.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FB-xVQKRJosU\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMICHAEL DES BARRES: \u003C\u002Fb\u003EThe highlights of Live Aid are not necessarily musical for me &#x2014; it was Dylan poking his head outside the trailer and seeing this iconoclastic group of groupies, who just happened to be world-renowned international rock stars. The irony of the stuff I saw remains indelibly seared into my DNA &#x2014; just seeing Dylan open that trailer door and looking at fans who happened to be multiplatinum artists, all gawking at him.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJIM KERR:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E I remember people like David Lee Roth running around like a mad man, and then in some quiet corner there&#x2019;d be a very elegant B.B. King, just sort of quietly plucking away. It was very much that kind of day. \u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EADAM ANT:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E There wasn&#x2019;t any record company people or business people backstage; the only people backstage were artists and the crew. That was it. They didn&#x2019;t even let managers backstage. It was literally a thing of beauty.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJOHN OATES: \u003C\u002Fb\u003EThe backstage area was just a bunch of trailers that were hastily set up in a semi-circle. Each trailer would have a piece of paper on it that had the band&#x2019;s name. There weren&#x2019;t enough trailers to accommodate all the artists. Basically, if you were performing, you had an hour before to be in your trailer, and then you had to vacate the trailer so another artist could come in. They were constantly walking around taking, like, Madonna&#x2019;s name off and putting Duran Duran&#x2019;s name up [on the trailer door]. Taking Neil Young&#x2019;s name off and putting Bob Dylan&#x2019;s name up. It was crazy. The amount of performers in one tiny semi-circle was just absolutely off the charts. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ETHOMAS DOLBY (London):\u003C\u002Fb\u003E After the Bowie set, I followed him up to the Royal Box. At its heart, Live Aid showed the world that all kinds of music can come together in harmony and bring focus and help to those who are in need. Gilmour, Pink Floyd&#x2026; Freddie Mercury, I remember backstage; there was one dressing room backstage where the band was getting ready, very minimal, one dressing room, no hanging about. It was quite extraordinary. Egos were out the window. It seemed to me that artists left with themselves, without the music industry around them, are just regular guys. You sit talking to people that you never imagined you&#x2019;d talk with, because you&#x2019;re all doing the same thing.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJOHN OATES:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E I remember there was a huge party at the hotel. Everywhere you turned, there was some kind of band, artist, or superstar. Everybody was there and everybody was hanging out. It can only be down to the genius of Mr. Bob Geldof, who had such a taste for both high and low that he was able to bring together such an exotic mix of talents, who all put aside their respective egos long enough to make a dent in the political map and relight the fire for activism in what had become a rather hedonistic and narcissistic industry. Having said that, I rather miss it.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMARTHA QUINN:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E Bob Geldof single-handedly changed the entire decade of the &apos;80s. One thing that always bugs me is when people call the &apos;80s the decade of greed, because it&#x2019;s so not true. This was the decade of the most philanthropy I think we&#x2019;ve ever seen! In the &apos;80s, giving was the new black.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ESTEVE NORMAN:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E Let&#x2019;s not forget the importance of Midge Ure&#x2019;s contribution. I just do not want to be seen to be riding on the coattails of something that was done for very, very different reasons, you know?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FkR9CiWyzcKw\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMICHAEL DES BARRES:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E To be in a band [Power Station] with [Duran Duran&#x2019;s] John and Andy Taylor, it was interesting to see these two young&apos;uns at the peak of Duran&#x2019;s fame almost tipping. It was a fascination to see it through their eyes. I could feel what they were feeling. It&#x2019;s a very difficult thing to put into words&#x2026; it was people who were reflective, insecure, overly hyped.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMARTHA QUINN:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E Duran Duran was kind of our One Direction. But now we know that they drove to JFK Stadium in total silence &#x2014; angry, weird, uncomfortable silence. Andy and Roger Taylor left [the group] after that. That original lineup didn&#x2019;t play together again until the next millennium. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FqXySC3xg0o4\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMICHAEL DES BARRES:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E The thing is about that that I found the most interesting is that you had all of these very nervous icons all in one place, so there was an energy there that was so unfamiliar, because you&#x2019;re used to seeing the posturing and the posing and the narcissism of these guys. But when they&#x2019;re all together under this unbelievable umbrella of this multibillion-people audience, there was so much nervousness going on. If I saw one person writing the words on the palm of his hand&#x2026; I saw 20 iconic rock &apos;n&#x2019; roll stars, boys and girls, writing lyrics, adjusting their bracelets, pulling the zips up on their leather jackets, and in Dylan and Woody&#x2019;s [Ron Wood&#x2019;s] case, playing three different songs at once when they got up there. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EADAM ANT:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E Elvis Costello wrote the lyrics to a Beatles song on his hand, I remember that.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FZB7-iOg516w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ETOM BAILEY: \u003C\u002Fb\u003EThe curtain went up and I had an enormous way to get to the microphone stand, and I knew that I had 32 bars to do it. I think I was playing it, but it wasn&#x2019;t going anywhere!\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FqhnHMiSlJ_g\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJIM KERR:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E For probably 80 to 90 percent of the time I was onstage, I was thinking, &#x201C;Holy f---, that&#x2019;s Jack Nicholson,&#x201D; so I don&#x2019;t really have much recollection of our set&#x2026; I was probably happy to get out of [then-wife] Chrissie [Hynde&#x2019;s] way for a quarter of an hour!\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EADAM ANT:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E I remember doing a really high jump and nearly killing myself when I landed. But I pulled it off.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJIM KERR:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E When I watch it now, two things come to my head instantly: &#x201C;Wow, there&#x2019;s Jack,&#x201D; and the other one is &#x201C;Oh God, wrong trousers.&#x201D; I wore these trousers, the kind of trousers someone who&#x2019;s gone yachting in Cape Cod would wear. I could feel them flapping like sailboats. I don&#x2019;t know what I was thinking, but they were \u003Ci\u003Enot\u003C\u002Fi\u003E outdoor onstage trousers. I just thought, &#x201C;Jim Morrison wouldn&#x2019;t wear these; this is not good.&#x201D; Any time I see a picture from Live Aid, there&#x2019;s a voice in my head that&#x2019;s saying, &#x201C;\u003Ci\u003EWrong trousers\u003C\u002Fi\u003E.&#x201D; They were like Dumbo&#x2019;s ears.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FFuAOl2oXXho\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ESTEVE NORMAN: \u003C\u002Fb\u003EIf I look back at it now and see the performance, there&#x2019;s four tunes and we just have bloody blank looks on our faces the whole time. I think we were terrified. The only thing I can remember of the time was jumping off the stage to run to the front and play the sax solo, and the rest kind of went by in a flash&#x2026; I look at my performance and I can sense that I&#x2019;m actually almost not there, you know? \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FFfV4AOgwY0o\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ETOM BAILEY\u003C\u002Fb\u003E: It was a frightening thing to take on. It was an enormous live audience and unbelievably enormous TV audience, and that could make you feel pretty nervous, knowing there was several million people watching.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJOHN OATES:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E In this day and age when media is all-pervasive and so global, a younger generation perhaps doesn&#x2019;t realize this was the first concert that was actually simulcast around the world. In this day of the Internet when you can access anything around the world at any moment, it probably seems kind of insignificant. But you don&#x2019;t realize how big of a deal it was back then. It was like going from the horse-and-carriage to the automobile or something. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJIM KERR:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E We were aware at the time that it was the biggest televised audience for anything in the world &#x2014; bigger than the moon landing, technology had changed so much.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMARTHA QUINN: \u003C\u002Fb\u003EBut honestly, [Duran Duran singer Simon Le Bon&#x2019;s voice crack in front of millions of people] wasn&#x2019;t even that bad. It&#x2019;s now become famous because they have talked about it a lot, but if they had just zipped their mouths about that, I don&#x2019;t think anybody would have ever noticed.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FmINh8ZOWSLw\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERICK SPRINGFIELD:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E It was a pretty surreal day. Everyone was thinking the world would change because of the incredible degree of commitment from so many for one cause. It didn&#x2019;t change anything. Music only reflects social change; it doesn&#x2019;t cause it. The problems in Africa have only multiplied a thousandfold. But it was a brave and noble try. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EADAM ANT: \u003C\u002Fb\u003EPolitics aside, the actual day itself was wonderful and it was a wonderful gesture; I think it became a promotional thing and just a bit of a mess, but I think at that time it was something that was really from the right place. Everybody did it from the heart. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMICHAEL DES BARRES:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E This Irish punk, Bobby Geldof, created this because he saw a documentary that infuriated him, and it began the idea that rock &apos;n&#x2019; roll stars could do something, that they could actually mean something. There was power there, and I think that that was probably the most important part of that event &#x2014; because God knows what happened to the money. It was a more innocent time. And a more stoned time. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJOHN OATES: \u003C\u002Fb\u003EIt definitely was a more innocent time. The &apos;80s were an era of what seemed to be endless possibility. It was like, the go-go &apos;80s, you know? And it filtered down through all aspects of society. Like Wall Street, people were making scads of money. Excess was everywhere. It seemed like the horizon was limitless. It just had that feeling to it. It was in between wars. It really had this euphoric kind of feeling. I think everyone&#x2019;s heart and intention was in the right the place with &#x201C;We Are the World&#x201D; and things like Live Aid. It was a nice attempt for the entertainment world and musicians in general to try to do something on a greater scale. There&#x2019;s nothing you can criticize about that. I would agree now, when you see the fact that Africa and so many places in the world are still in the state that they&#x2019;re in, it really was only a Band-Aid on a wound that is very hard to heal. I think people are more realistic now. People have more ability to access information. In those days, you just didn&#x2019;t know; you felt if you raised some money and you could do something good, then good things would happen. It wasn&#x2019;t that good things didn&#x2019;t happen, it just that it wasn&#x2019;t enough. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EADAM ANT:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E I would have suggested that the U.N. take the money in. I think a lot of the food got to the borders and got taken by the army. And the actual government itself in the problem areas said it did cause a lot of trouble, because a lot of people moved to centers to try and get to the food, and it wasn&#x2019;t there; it had been stolen by the army. It&#x2019;s been quite sad, very sad. But I think on the day itself, the energy was there.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMIDGE URE:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E I can tell you off the top of my head that between Band Aid and Live Aid, I think it generated somewhere in the region of $190 million. So it generated a substantial amount of money; I think the record initially raised something like 7 million pounds.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EJOHN OATES: \u003C\u002Fb\u003ETo me, you can&#x2019;t worry about how much money was raised. What&#x2019;s [the] more important question is, &#x201C;How much money actually got to where it was needed to go?&#x201D; That&#x2019;s the real question. That&#x2019;s the question everyone should ask any time they get involved with a charity or a benefit type of event. How much of the funds are being siphoned off on the bureaucratic side of the organization or the event? How much filters out to where it&#x2019;s supposed to go?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMARTHA QUINN: \u003C\u002Fb\u003EWhere the money is going &#x2014; that is a totally valid question. I support that question. But does that mean we shouldn&#x2019;t \u003Ci\u003Etry\u003C\u002Fi\u003E? I love that our gut reaction to suffering is to help. The vibe at Live Aid was so positive. Everyone donated their time and paid their own expenses. Philadelphia gave JFK Stadium for free! It was not only a dizzying array of talent, but everything good about humanity. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EGLENN TIPTON, JUDAS PRIEST \u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003Cb\u003E(Philadelphia): \u003C\u002Fb\u003ETo be amongst so many respected and talented artists who were all fighting the same cause was an incredible feeling and one that will live with me for the rest of my life. When everyone joined together onstage and sang &#x201C;We Are the World,&#x201D; it was one of the most emotional moments I can recall. And knowing that music, even heavy metal, played its part was a wonderful feeling.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Ciframe width=\"560\" height=\"315\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.youtube.com\u002Fembed\u002FkcqkA4A16dU\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMIDGE URE:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E Here we are nearly 30 years later, and [&#x201C;Do They Know It&#x2019;s Christmas?&#x201D;] is still played on the radio; the song royalties go directly to the Band Aid trust and go directly to Africa.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EHOWARD JONES (London):\u003C\u002Fb\u003E Live Aid was the highlight event of the &apos;80s. It brought together music artists and a global audience,&#xA0;for a great cause.&#xA0;It was proof that one person&#x2019;s outrage could be transformed into something that brought the world together and could save thousands of people&#x2019;s lives. It was a privilege to be part of it. I hope that with this 30th anniversary, it is remembered as a benchmark for global activism and compassion.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ESTEVE NORMAN:\u003C\u002Fb\u003E It seems so weird it was that 30 years ago. Good grief.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EFollow Lyndsey on&#xA0;\u003Ca 87be75ef9e174a3214ecd89226ebf9.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":250,"height":333,"size_type":"250","formats":{}},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w300\u002F31535319ef87be75ef9e174a3214ecd89226ebf9.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":300,"height":400,"size_type":"300","formats":{}},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w350\u002F31535319ef87be75ef9e174a3214ecd89226ebf9.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":350,"height":467,"size_type":"350","formats":{}},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w400\u002F31535319ef87be75ef9e174a3214ecd89226ebf9.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":400,"height":533,"size_type":"400","formats":{}},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w450\u002F31535319ef87be75ef9e174a3214ecd89226ebf9.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":450,"height":600,"size_type":"450","formats":{}},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002F31535319ef87be75ef9e174a3214ecd89226ebf9.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":500,"height":667,"size_type":"500","formats":{}},{"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002F31535319ef87be75ef9e174a3214ecd89226ebf9.jpg","type":"image\u002Fjpeg","width":540,"height":720,"size_type":"original","formats":{}}],"url":"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002Foriginal\u002FMCYTCftXZ0ydOM0pL_N3Htrua20","source_url":"http:\u002F\u002Fmagazines.zenfs.com\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002Foriginal\u002FMCYTCftXZ0ydOM0pL_N3Htrua20"}],"highlight_color":"#000000","data_version":"1","title":"Back to Black: Remembering Meeting Amy Winehouse","summary":"This article originally ran on July 23, 2011, the day that Amy Winehouse died at age 27. We are re-running it today in honor of the theatrical release of the acclaimed documentary 'AMY.'","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EThis article originally ran on Yahoo Music on July 23, 2011, the day that Amy Winehouse died at age 27. We are re-running it today in honor of the theatrical release of the acclaimed documentary \u003Ci\u003EAMY\u003C\u002Fi\u003E.\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBack in March 2007, Yahoo Music went to South By Southwest, the annual Austin music festival where so many up-and-coming artists flock to kickstart their careers in America, and booked a studio where SXSW 2007&#x2019;s buzziest buzz acts came through to perform for the Yahoo cameras. All sorts of impressive musicians stopped by &#x2014; Mika, Peter Bjorn &amp; John, Albert Hammond Jr. of the Strokes, Brandi Carlile. But the moment I remember most from that whirlwind weekend is, unsurprisingly, when the infamously rehab-resistant, beehived British bad girl, Amy Winehouse, sauntered through the door.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E \n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002Fbcd4b82e998628acbc874e554ad3587b8a39986f.jpg\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" alt=\"Back to Black: Remembering Meeting Amy Winehouse\"\u003Ephotos: Neal Weiss \n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EPart of what made Amy&#x2019;s appearance at Yahoo&#x2019;s SXSW headquarters that hot Austin day so memorable was the fact that she showed up at all. While Amy wasn&#x2019;t quite a superstar yet, she&#x2019;d already established a reputation for being troubled, flaky, and living up to her apparently autobiographical hit, &#x201C;Rehab.&#x201D; That year at SXSW she was probably the most hyped act of the entire festival, and she was scheduled to perform at a series of high-profile parties and showcases, but she ended up canceling about half of her appearances. So my fellow Yahoo staffers and I weren&#x2019;t entirely convinced that our shoot would go on as planned, until we actually saw Amy arrive.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBut when Amy finally arrived, oh boy, she arrived in style. The scene was like one of those old &#x2018;80s commercials for E.F. Hutton. Everything stopped. Everyone fell silent. A star, albeit a somewhat tarnished one, had undeniably entered the room. This was not a normal person.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERelated: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Fmusic\u002Famy-winehouse-documentary-premiere-illuminates-the-122675533226.html\"\u003EAmy Winehouse Documentary Premiere Illuminates the Life of a Brilliant But Troubled Talent\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EDressed in skinny jeans (and I do mean skinny) and a sleeveless tee that showed off her copiously tattooed skinny arms, balancing a bouffant on her lollipop head that looked like it weighed more than she did, and compulsively sucking on an actual lollipop while simultaneously shuffling a deck of cards from one hand to another, Amy looked like T-R-O-U-B-L-E. But I liked her straight away. When she shook my hand, she gave me a little wink that almost made it seem like she was flirting with me &#x2014; and I enjoyed it. She came across like the leader of some tough-as-acrylic-nails girl gang &#x2014; like \u003Ci\u003EGrease\u003C\u002Fi\u003E&#x2019;s Rizzo, only British &#x2014; and man, I wanted to join that gang. Amy was, in a word, cool.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E \n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fg\u002Fimages\u002Fspaceball.gif\" width=\"540\" height=\"720\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002Fb9b155ee1d0c17375049133786ca53af6fee6843.jpg\"\u003E \n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EStill, it was obvious she was a bit of a mess, and maybe very fragile behind her misleading tatted-up, tough-girl exterior. She explained that she sucked on her ubiquitous lollipops to ease her cravings as she tried to quit smoking. She explained that fidgeting with that deck of cards eased her nerves after breaking up with her boyfriend the night before. (I \u003Ci\u003Ebelieve\u003C\u002Fi\u003E the boyfriend in question was chef\u002Fmusician Alex Clare, not her infamous future ex-husband later known to the world as &#x201C;Blake Incarcerated.&#x201D;) She then went outside, sat on the sidewalk, shed her tank top, and kicked back on the concrete to bask in the Texas sun &#x2014; in her bra. And this was not a bra that could pass for half-shirt or swimsuit top, but a full-on lace-trimmed, hook-and-eye-backed, adjustable-strapped, underwired, hospital-white garment straight out of Marks &amp; Spencer&#x2019;s lingerie section. Sitting there in broad daylight on the highly foot-trafficked street, in her brassiere and winged eyeliner and not much else, Amy seemed oblivious as tourists strolled by and gawked at the beehive-topped spectacle on the grimy pavement. She just didn&#x2019;t care. She was just too cool to care. Or she was too&#x2026; \u003Ci\u003Esomething\u003C\u002Fi\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EHer interview was a wreck too, admittedly. While I was not the one conducting the interview, thankfully, I was in the room, and it was downright painful to watch as my well-meaning and well-prepared co-worker asked her question after valid question, yet she answered monosyllabically and almost defensively while he squirmed. There was so much uncomfortable silence between the questions and answers, I could actually hear the electrical hum of the video equipment and studio lights. Nothing about Amy seemed to come easy. She was a complicated woman, to put it mildly.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-embed tmblr-full\"\u003E \n \u003Ciframe width=\"540\" height=\"303\" allowfullscreen data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmusic.yahoo.com\u002Fvideo\u002Famy-winehouse-2007-vintage-interview-213049810.html?format=embed\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fiframe\u003E \n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBut then&#x2026; Amy&#x2019;s acoustic guitarist joined her. And Amy sang. And that is when everything fell into place. Singing seemed to be the one thing that came easy to Amy.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EIn her tragic later years, Amy became known for her shambolic performances (and her canceled ones), but that fine SXSW day, she was magic, not tragic. She magnificently belted out &#x201C;Rehab&#x201D; and &#x201C;You Know I&#x2019;m No Good,&#x201D; nailing each song in one take, and her whiskey-gargled voice was one of the most unique I&#x2019;d ever heard. Accompanied by nothing but her guitar player and doing nothing but sitting on a stool with her heavily mascara&#x2019;d eyes half-closed, she exhibited more presence and true star quality than most pop starlets manage with all of their costume changes, backup dancers, and multi-tracked vocal effects.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E \n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fg\u002Fimages\u002Fspaceball.gif\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002Fa1c9fc68b74385498ccc1619034932bc45e438e4.jpg\"\u003E \n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EAmy was clearly the real deal, and it was actually \u003Ci\u003Ebecause\u003C\u002Fi\u003E she was such a mess that her music connected. It was because tunes like &#x201C;Rehab&#x201D; and &#x201C;You Know I&#x2019;m No Good&#x201D; weren&#x2019;t novelty songs, and weren&#x2019;t just recorded for shock value, but were real-messy-life tales pulled from the wine-and-nicotine-stained pages of her own fascinating diary.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERelated: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Fmusic\u002Fwatch-a-14-year-old-amy-winehouse-sing-happy-122785649051.html\"\u003EWatch a 14-Year-Old Amy Winehouse Sing &#x2018;Happy Birthday&#x2019; Like No Other in New Documentary \u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EI never saw Amy Winehouse perform again after that special day. Most of her SXSW gigs that weekend never happened, and my plans to see her play the following week at the Spaceland club in Los Angeles fell through when she canceled that appearance the day of the show. (I still have the tickets; either I&#x2019;d over-optimistically hoped that there&#x2019;d be a makeup date someday, or deep down, I knew those tickets would one day have historical value.) Later on I attended the V Festival in England and California&#x2019;s Coachella fest in hopes of seeing her rock a proper large stage, but she pulled out of those events at the last minute, too. And now, I&#x2019;ll never have the chance to see her sing again.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EBut perhaps it is for the best that I only recall Amy in her prime &#x2014; stripped (both literally and acoustically) and just singing her tattooed heart out, before she became better known for her tabloid marriage, tabloid divorce, bar brawls, drug arrests, court appearances, rehab stints, and failed comeback attempts. Because once she started careening along her seemingly unstoppable downward spiral, she no longer seemed so &#x201C;cool&#x201D; anymore. Now, she was just plain sad. I wouldn&#x2019;t have wanted to witness a travesty like her disastrous Serbia concert [in June 2011], when she hit the stage an hour late and was inebriated to the point of being unable to sing an entire chorus. (Witnesses claimed bodyguards pushed her onstage and forced her to perform, despite her condition.) The Serbian press dubbed that concert &#x201C;the worst in the history of Belgrade,&#x201D; and her European tour was subsequently canceled.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ERelated: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Fmusic\u002Fflashback-mark-ronson-remembers-the-making-of-107945605406.html\"\u003EMark Ronson Remembers the Making of &#x2018;Valerie&#x2019;\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EI was, I admit, hoping that her tour&#x2019;s cancelation would give Amy time to rest, recuperate, and yes, go to rehab again.. .because I&#x2019;d never given up hope that she would finally get it together and record a follow-up to \u003Ci\u003EBack to Black\u003C\u002Fi\u003E. Amy had been frustratingly missing in action since that album swept the Grammys in early 2008, and during her lengthy hiatus other rebel-divas, like Britain&#x2019;s similarly soulful Duffy, Florence Welch, Jessie J, and especially Adele, and the similarly sassy Lady Gaga, Kesha, and Katy Perry, had all come along and stolen her thunder. Snooki from \u003Ci\u003EJersey Shore\u003C\u002Fi\u003E practically even stole her hairstyle. It was starting to seem like Amy&#x2019;s career had gone back to black, for good &#x2014; yet I still believed she had it in her to record another Grammy-worthy classic and reclaim her old glory. Sadly, as we all know now, that was not meant to be. Mitch Winehouse, Amy&#x2019;s taxi-driving father, actually put out a new album out before she did.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E \n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fg\u002Fimages\u002Fspaceball.gif\" width=\"540\" height=\"720\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002F31535319ef87be75ef9e174a3214ecd89226ebf9.jpg\"\u003E \n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EAmy&#x2019;s final released recording during her lifetime was for Quincy Jones&#x2019;s all-star album \u003Ci\u003EQ: Soul Bossa Nostra\u003C\u002Fi\u003E in 2010; while that track reunited her with Mark Ronson, the superproducer who helmed \u003Ci\u003EBack to Black\u003C\u002Fi\u003E and made her a star, the product of this reunion, a cover of Lesley Gore&#x2019;s &#x201C;It&#x2019;s My Party (And I&#x2019;ll Cry If I Want To),&#x201D; didn&#x2019;t come close to the greatness of anything on \u003Ci\u003EBack to Black\u003C\u002Fi\u003E (or Amy&#x2019;s remake of the Zutons&#x2019; &#x201C;Valerie,&#x201D; arguably the best cut on Ronson&#x2019;s 2007 covers album, \u003Ci\u003EVersion\u003C\u002Fi\u003E). Amy sounded stinking drunk, or just not at all lucid, on the slurry recording, which was widely panned.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003ETruly, Amy Winehouse should be remembered the way I choose to remember her, based on my one encounter with her on a Texas sidewalk in 2007: as a very troubled, but very talented, old soul.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EAnd yes, she \u003Ci\u003Ewas\u003C\u002Fi\u003E cool.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003E\u003Ci\u003EAMY\u003C\u002Fi\u003E premieres this weekend in theaters nationwide.\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EFollow Lyndsey on \u003Ca
